        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer425574247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3708706710795693514">This is one of the best books I've ever read. So brilliant, in fact, I find it hard to describe why I LOVE it so much! The author evokes the emotions of his characters with unique brilliance, using a stream of conscious style of writing to describe inner dialogue, so that we feel exactly what each character feels, especially at moments of great stress. Not only this, but the characters themselves are infinitely well-drawn and their actions believable, totally sympathetic and consistent throughou</span>
  <span id="freeText3708706710795693514" style="display:none">This is one of the best books I've ever read. So brilliant, in fact, I find it hard to describe why I LOVE it so much! The author evokes the emotions of his characters with unique brilliance, using a stream of conscious style of writing to describe inner dialogue, so that we feel exactly what each character feels, especially at moments of great stress. Not only this, but the characters themselves are infinitely well-drawn and their actions believable, totally sympathetic and consistent throughout. The descriptions of what it was like to serve in the First World War are also vivid and again (I think) unique in that they describe not just the horror but also the constant organisational effort involved in moving troops about, and sometimes just the sheer boredom of it all.<br />Finally, the descriptions of the English countryside - a vanishing landscape in the early 20th century - are just perfect. The scene where Christopher Tietjens (the hero) and Valentine Wannop fall in love whilst riding a horse and cart all night through thick fog was so real for me I wished I could actually be in it.<br />I wish I could write like Ford Madox Ford. That's all I can say.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3708706710795693514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_410598299" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410598299" />
    <a title="Ka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3776311-ka"><img alt="Ka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282082626p2/3776311.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/410598299?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ka" href="/user/show/3776311-ka">Ka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3776311-ka?shelf=wwi">wwi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3776311-ka?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3776311-ka?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer410598299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9455538511887048964">I can't decide whether to give this book 2 stars or 4. Ultimately it does succeed as a powerful story of the effects of the Great War on English society. Instead of the sweeping narrative of the typical war novel, FMF takes his story completely inside the characters' heads, looking at society and war in the microcosm, an approach that must be respected. <br/><br/>And yet. I did not enjoy reading it. The third book does finally portray a good bit of the misery and danger of the trenches and the front lines</span>
  <span id="freeText9455538511887048964" style="display:none">I can't decide whether to give this book 2 stars or 4. Ultimately it does succeed as a powerful story of the effects of the Great War on English society. Instead of the sweeping narrative of the typical war novel, FMF takes his story completely inside the characters' heads, looking at society and war in the microcosm, an approach that must be respected. <br /><br />And yet. I did not enjoy reading it. The third book does finally portray a good bit of the misery and danger of the trenches and the front lines. The first two books are really more about a love triangle in England during the time of the first war. At all times, the story is narrated through the inner thoughts, relevant or irrelevant, of the 3 main characters. Often these are overwrought; it is exhausting to read so many sentences ending in exclamation points. It's like a soap opera, in that you can skip forward 30 pages and be confident you'll still be in the same scene. The reader is left to tease out the few narrative points amidst the torrent of banality. <br /><br />I did not read the fourth book, choosing to side with Conrad and others who called it 'a disaster' and who state that FMF never intended it to be published. <br /><br />A further disadvantage of the extreme interior view FMF gives, of the war and the times, is that context is never developed or revealed. I am not sure whether, for example, I would have perceived the portrayal of the disintegration of classes, had I not already known that WWI was the catalyst for this. I can't imagine what a reader without prior knowledge of the era and the war specifically would make of it. <br /><br />Editorial addition after watching the HBO miniseries of Parade's End: It pairs perfectly with the book. How often can that be said?! The book is not terribly visual, while the miniseries is lush with color &amp; period detail, supplying the images the book didn't. The TV series, of course, could not contain the pages and pages of interior reflection that make up 2/3 of the book, so it leaves out the part I found most tiresome. At the same time, there were many times in the miniseries when I thought, 'I sure am glad I read the book and know everything that was meant by that 15-second scene'. And revisiting the scenes &amp; characters from the book was like finding valued old friends. I now think, or maybe I always did, that Christopher Tietjens is one of the great literary characters. Thanks to the symbiosis of book &amp; movie, I'm upgrading yo 4 stars. <br />A final comment on the version - I bought an inexpensive ($2.99) Kindle version, which was very sloppily converted and suffered far more than the usual 'Kindleisms' of sentences illogically broken and scrambled words. The book would benefit greatly by well done commentary &amp; footnotes, and it would be worth it to pay more for them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9455538511887048964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_289630680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/289630680" />
    <a title="Aliza" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/861139-aliza"><img alt="Aliza" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578599623p2/861139.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/289630680?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aliza" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aliza" href="/user/show/861139-aliza">Aliza</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer289630680" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16252342198941567413">This novel has touches of genius but is at its core deeply flawed, an example of execution failing to match talent or intent. There are parts that come alive - in particular, anything involving Sylvia, who is monstrous but also fascinating and even strangely relatable. But the war sections drag and while I understand and appreciate modernist techniques, perhaps I didn't care enough Christopher Tietjens to put the effort into truly focusing on those chapters. The final book feels like it is entir</span>
  <span id="freeText16252342198941567413" style="display:none">This novel has touches of genius but is at its core deeply flawed, an example of execution failing to match talent or intent. There are parts that come alive - in particular, anything involving Sylvia, who is monstrous but also fascinating and even strangely relatable. But the war sections drag and while I understand and appreciate modernist techniques, perhaps I didn't care enough Christopher Tietjens to put the effort into truly focusing on those chapters. The final book feels like it is entirely separate, both in style and story, and I had to push myself to finish it. Interesting characters are simply dropped, their fates barely addressed. And while I did find Sylvia fascinating, I was bothered by the fact that the women are all monsters bent on destroying their men or pure, innocent saviors who sacrifice all for the men they love. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16252342198941567413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1986547506262693957">This book is a tetralogy composed by the following books: Some Do Not (1924), No More Parades (1925), A Man Could Stand UP (1926) and Last Post (1928).<br/><br/>Even if these four novels have been reissued in 1948, after Second World War, the first omnibus version was published by Knopf in 1950.<br/><br/>It is interesting to find out that I have the same opinion as stated by Graham Greene: "an afterthought which he (Ford) had not intended to write and later regretted having written.” In addition, "...the Last Post </span>
  <span id="freeText1986547506262693957" style="display:none">This book is a tetralogy composed by the following books: Some Do Not (1924), No More Parades (1925), A Man Could Stand UP (1926) and Last Post (1928).<br><br>Even if these four novels have been reissued in 1948, after Second World War, the first omnibus version was published by Knopf in 1950.<br><br>It is interesting to find out that I have the same opinion as stated by Graham Greene: "an afterthought which he (Ford) had not intended to write and later regretted having written.” In addition, "...the Last Post was more than a mistake—it was a disaster, a disaster which has delayed a full critical appreciation of Parade's End."<br><br>Lets go back then to the review of the four novels.<br><br>In the fist book of this series, the author describes the main character, Christopher Tietjens, “the last English <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tory" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Tory</a>” after the World War I and his involvement with two women in his life: his faithless wife, Sylvia (a quite annoying woman in my opinion) and his lover, Valentine Wannop, a pacifist and suffragette.<br><br>In the second volume, the author describes the Chistopher’s engagement in the Great War, who lives his aristocratic world and moves to the chaos of the French trenches.<br><br>The third volume - A Man Could Stand Up, is the best one in my opinion. It starts with the Armistice Day and the author describes the feelings of the main characters in a magnificent way. The author also describes Christopher’s emotional and psychological responses under fire. As consequence, he decides to retreat and decides to live with Valentine, selling antiques as a way of economically survive.<br><br>And the last one, the worst of this series, does have almost nothing to do with the previous ones. It seems that the author was looking for some kind of redemption or some other feeling which we cannot fully understand.<br><br>All four novels area available at the Public Domain:<br><br>Free download available at <a href="http://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/f/ford/ford_madox/some-do-not/" rel="nofollow noopener">eBooks@Adelaide</a>.<br><br>Free download available at <a href="http://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/f/ford/ford_madox/no-more-parades/" rel="nofollow noopener">eBooks@Adelaide</a>.<br><br>Free download available at <a href="http://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/f/ford/ford_madox/man-could-stand-up/" rel="nofollow noopener">eBooks@Adelaide</a>.<br><br>Free download available at <a href="http://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/f/ford/ford_madox/last-post/" rel="nofollow noopener">eBooks@Adelaide</a>.<br><br>In 2012, HBO, BBC, and VRT produced a television adaptation <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt1956017/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Parade's End (2012)</a> written by Tom Stoppard and starring Benedict Cumberbatch and Rebecca Hall.<br><br><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404611690i/10264075.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>3* The Good Soldier<br>4* Some Do Not<br>4* No More Parades<br>4* A Man Could Stand Up<br>2* Last Post<br>TR The Fifth Queen<br>TR The Young Lovell<br>TR The Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood<br>TR Henry James, a critical study<br></span>

<div id="review_82655724" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82655724" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82655724?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679417286" href="/book/show/850621.Parade_s_End">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=everymans-library">everymans-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82655724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7638882127887129257">“At the beginning of the war…I had to look in on the War Office, and in a room I found a fellow…What do you think he was doing…what the hell do you think he was doing? He was devising the ceremonial for the disbanding of a Kitchener battalion. You can’t say we were not prepared in one matter at least…. Well, the end of the show was to be: the adjutant would stand the battalion at ease; the band would play Land of Hope and Glory, and then the adjutant would say: There will be no more parades…. Do</span>
  <span id="freeText7638882127887129257" style="display:none">“At the beginning of the war…I had to look in on the War Office, and in a room I found a fellow…What do you think he was doing…what the hell do you think he was doing? He was devising the ceremonial for the disbanding of a Kitchener battalion. You can’t say we were not prepared in one matter at least…. Well, the end of the show was to be: the adjutant would stand the battalion at ease; the band would play Land of Hope and Glory, and then the adjutant would say: There will be no more parades…. Don’t you see how symbolical it was—the band playing Land of Hope and Glory, and then the adjutant saying: There will be no more parades?… For there won’t. There won’t, there damn well won’t. No more Hope, no more Glory, no more parades for you and me any more. Nor for the country…nor for the world, I dare say… None… Gone… Napoo finny! No…more…parades!”<br /><br /><br />Ford Madox Ford made his reputation as a novelist on the war &amp; peace themes. <i>The Good Soldier</i> (1915) is his most famous, and is on several different ‘Best 100 Novels of the Century’ lists, as is his four-part <i>Parade's End</i>. The latter book was recently in the news, Tom Stoppard having just completed his adaptation for BBC television, the mini-series scheduled to air in 2011. This novel reminds me of other great chronicles of individual lives and war, in this case a chronicle of the life of Christopher Tietjens, "the last Tory," a brilliant government statistician from a wealthy land-owning family who is serving in the British Army during World War I. While this is generally considered a "war" novel it is unique in the way Ford has Tietjens' consciousness taking primacy over the war-events like a filter. Ford constructs a protagonist for whom the war is but one aspect of his life, and not always even the most prominent though he is in the middle of it. The two central novels follow Tietjens in the army in France and Belgium as he ruminates on how to be a better soldier and untangle his strange social life. In a narrative beginning before the war and ending after the armistice, Ford's project is to situate an unimaginable cataclysm within a social, moral and psychological complexity. The result is a modern literary project that rivals those of Anthony Powell's <i>A Dance to the Music of Time</i> or, more aptly, Evelyn Waugh's <i>Sword of Honour</i> trilogy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7638882127887129257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1531432433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1531432433" />
    <a title="Beth Bonini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini"><img alt="Beth Bonini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444482564p2/3106785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1531432433?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Bonini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Bonini" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini">Beth Bonini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=bad-marriage">bad-marriage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=mental-health">mental-health</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1531432433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6208636746264731820">I usually try to stick to a policy of only reviewing novels that I have 'recently' (meaning days, or weeks at most) read, but I'm going to make an exception in this case. Having loved the BBC production, with Benedict Cumberbatch as protagonist Christopher Tietjens, I wanted to read the novel in order to fill in the gaps and details that I felt I might not have grasped. (Do other people do this? My typical response, after watching a film adapted from a novel, is to want to read the novel.) After</span>
  <span id="freeText6208636746264731820" style="display:none">I usually try to stick to a policy of only reviewing novels that I have 'recently' (meaning days, or weeks at most) read, but I'm going to make an exception in this case. Having loved the BBC production, with Benedict Cumberbatch as protagonist Christopher Tietjens, I wanted to read the novel in order to fill in the gaps and details that I felt I might not have grasped. (Do other people do this? My typical response, after watching a film adapted from a novel, is to want to read the novel.) After spending much of January 2014 slogging through this book -- and SLOG it was, despite many wonderful moments and bits of writing -- I realised two things: Tom Stoppard and Benedict Cumberbatch are both geniuses. If you felt that the television drama was somewhat elliptical, please believe me when I tell you that it is a masterpiece of straightforward narration compared to the novel. And if you thought Tietjens was a maddening, inscrutable character, let me assure you that Cumberbatch -- through his extremely expressive face and noble suffering -- managed to make that character sympathetic (even lovable) in a way that really doesn't come across in the novel. I don't regret reading this novel, but if you are short of time, do go straight to the BBC production without feeling the slightest bit guilty. You will also be able to enjoy the sumptuous scenery, costumes and acting.<br /><br />Personal note: I was motivated to write this slight review, or rather recommendation, after reading Stravinsky's Lunch. In discussing Stella Bowen -- Ford's partner, and the inspiration for Valentine Wannop -- I got an interesting insight into Ford as an author and a man. He was no Christopher Tietjens . . . but it did explain the bizarre scene on the train between Tietjens and Sylvia (the one that led to their disastrous marriage). I had never been able to believe that Tietjens would have sex on a train with a woman he barely knew, no matter how seductive. I suspect that was more Fordian. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6208636746264731820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1687600242" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1687600242" />
    <a title="Emilia Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32845594-emilia-barnes"><img alt="Emilia Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404673186p2/32845594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1687600242?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilia Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilia Barnes" href="/user/show/32845594-emilia-barnes">Emilia Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141392196" href="/book/show/13561867-parade-s-end">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=genre-historical">genre-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=genre-drama">genre-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=period-edwardian">period-edwardian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=genre-classic">genre-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=theme-politics">theme-politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=period-world-war-i">period-world-war-i</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1687600242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9936000106270943844">If you Google Image search and type in "stiff upper lip gif", the first two images you will find are:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1592630735i/29676051.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1592630735ra/29676052.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This is Benedict Cumberbatch playing Parade's End's main character, Christopher Tietjens. <br/><br/>Anyway, full confession, I did not read the last volume, called the Last Post, of this tetralogy. I had every intention of reading it, but: <br/><br/>a) By the time I came to the ending of A Man Could Stand Up <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[and it was a really beautiful ending <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I just couldn't bear to see the story go </span>
  <span id="freeText9936000106270943844" style="display:none">If you Google Image search and type in "stiff upper lip gif", the first two images you will find are:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1592630735i/29676051.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1592630735ra/29676052.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is Benedict Cumberbatch playing Parade's End's main character, Christopher Tietjens. <br><br>Anyway, full confession, I did not read the last volume, called the Last Post, of this tetralogy. I had every intention of reading it, but: <br><br>a) By the time I came to the ending of A Man Could Stand Up <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and it was a really beautiful ending <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I just couldn't bear to see the story go on. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I just wanted Tietjens to live in fiction's happily ever after heaven in my imagination. Ford has made him suffer for me for some 700 pages by that point. I honestly couldn't bear to see Sylvia take one more stab at him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>and<br><br>b) There is some justification in omitting the Last Post in that, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parade%27s_End#cite_note-11" rel="nofollow noopener">as Graham Greene wrote</a>: it's "an afterthought which he (Ford) had not intended to write and later regretted having written." (I don't know if this is true or not, but certainly the ending of a Man Could Stand Up gave me every kind of closure I needed to feel that I have read a complete story.)<br><br>As to the three volumes I did read, this was an excellent novel. The modernist style in which it is written at times obfuscates clarity, but for the most part it rendered a heavy subject matter relatively easy to read. But YMMV of course. In this particular case I think it helped Ford express the clash and horror of the change that the First World War thrust onto a society which was already ending anyway (as his main character, Tietjens, is fully aware of). <br><br>Tietjens is, mentally speaking, an 18th century gentleman, with stubborn ways which he sticks to, which further his absolute ruin at the hands of those who have already moved on. The brilliance of this lies in the nuanced observation of what being the "Last Tory" (as Tietjens is repeatedly described) actually means in this new world - you'd assume that this would mean a backward idea of shooting poaching peasants in the face and subjugating women around you, but that's not in fact what Ford means with that description, and Tietjens doesn't see the world in that way either. One of my favourite parts was when Tietjens and Miss Wannop discuss the suffragette cause, which shows his view of the world better than anything.<br><br><blockquote>'[...]But just get it out, will you? Say once and for all that--you know the proper, pompous manner: you are not without sympathy with our aims: but you disapprove--oh, immensely, strongly--of our methods.'<br><br>It struck Tietjens that the young woman was a good deal more interested in the cause--of votes for women--than he had given her credit for. <br><br>'I don't. I approve entirely of your methods: but your aims are idiotic.'<br><br>'You don't know, I suppose, that Gertie Wilson, who's in bed at our house, is wanted by the police: not only for yesterday, but for putting explosives in a whole series of letter-boxes?'<br><br>'I didn't...but it was a perfectly proper thing to do. She hasn't burned any of my letters or I might be annoyed; but it wouldn't interfere with my approval.'<br><br>'Look here. Don't be one of those ignoble triflers who say the vote won't do women any good. Women have a rotten time. They do, really. If you'd seen what I've seen, I'm not talking through my hat.' Her voice became quite deep: she had tears in her eyes: 'Poor women do!' she said, 'little insignificant creatures. We've got to change the divorce laws. We've got to get better conditions. You couldn't stand it if you knew what I know.'<br><br>'I daresay I shouldn't. But I don't know, so I can!' <br><br>She said with deep disappointment:'Oh, you are a beast! And I shall never beg your pardon for saying that. I don't believe you mean what you say, but merely to say it is heartless. [...] You don't know the case of the Pimlico army clothing factory workers or you wouldn't say the vote would be no use to women.'<br><br>'I know the case perfectly well,' Tietjens said: 'It came under my official notice, and I remember thinking that there never was a more signal instance of the uselessness of the vote to anyone.'<br><br>'We can't be thinking of the same case,' she said.<br><br>'We are,' he answered. 'The Pimlico army clothing factory is in the constituency of Westminster; the Under-Secretary for War is member for Westminster; his majority at the last election was six hundred. The clothing factory employed seven hundred men at 1s. 6d. an hour, all these men having votes in Westminster. The seven hundred men wrote to the Under-Secretary to say that if their screw wasn't raised to two bob they'd vote solid against him at the next election...'<br><br>Miss Wannop said: 'Well then!'<br><br>'So,' Tietjens said: 'The Under-Secretary had the seven hundred men at eighteenpence fired and took on seven hundred women at tenpence. What good did the vote do the seven hundred men? What good did a vote ever do anyone?'<br><br>Miss Wannop checked at that and Tietjens prevented her exposure of his fallacy by saying quickly:<br><br>'Now, if the seven hundred women, backed by all the other ill-used, sweated women of the country, had threatened the Under-Secretary, burned the pillar-boxes, and cut up all the golf greens round his country house, they'd have had their wages raised to half a crown next week. That's the only straight method. It's the feudal system at work.'</blockquote><br><br>And yet, although Tietjens has a genius way of seeing the world with perfect clarity, and seeing how he himself is absolutely unfitted to prosper in it, he cannot abandon his principles and continuously makes decisions that make life more difficult for him. <br><br>The first book, Some Do Not... is perhaps my favourite. The other two volumes were harder to read, often because the style of writing didn't help to provide clarity as to what was happening and why, when you're in the middle of a war and everything is in an uncomfortable chaos.<br><br>I can't recommend this book to everybody. I can see that for some it will be quite impossible to read. But for me, who loves history, and who is passionately interested in English society, this certainly was a must read.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="9936000106270943844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_373415668" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/373415668" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6136140-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408733495p2/6136140.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/373415668?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/6136140-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307744203" href="/book/show/11490042-parade-s-end">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6136140-lauren?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer373415668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17190222610621832616">I enjoyed this a lot more than I expected! I decided to read the books before the BBC miniseries came out, and I'm really glad that I did. Ford has created a wonderful character in Christopher Tietjens - noble to a fault, stubborn, fiercely smart, stiff and ponderous on the outside and a big teddy bear on the inside. You love him even when you want to slap him and tell him he's messing it all up. His wife Sylvia is fascinatingly manipulative, and even though she's one of the most genuinely terri</span>
  <span id="freeText17190222610621832616" style="display:none">I enjoyed this a lot more than I expected! I decided to read the books before the BBC miniseries came out, and I'm really glad that I did. Ford has created a wonderful character in Christopher Tietjens - noble to a fault, stubborn, fiercely smart, stiff and ponderous on the outside and a big teddy bear on the inside. You love him even when you want to slap him and tell him he's messing it all up. His wife Sylvia is fascinatingly manipulative, and even though she's one of the most genuinely terrible people I've ever read about, you still manage to care for her, too. Valentine, Tietjens' love interest, is a whipsmart suffragette whose temperament is a far better match for him than Sylvia. Ford does a great job of giving these characters a voice - I particularly enjoyed reading the chapters from Sylvia's perspective. To everyone else she's a villainous whore - from her own perspective she's a mischief maker, and her schemes are hilariously well-planned. <br /><br />The books do drag at certain points - I had a lot of trouble getting into the second book, because the first was so focused on the will-they-won't-they relationship between Tietjens and Valentine...then book 2 starts and it's all about warfare. I had some trouble adjusting to the shift in tone. Ford also has an annoying habit of introducing a character purely for the sake of affecting the plot, then giving us a 2-3 page life story for this new character when we're never going to see them again. Why yes, Ford, let's hear aaaallllll about the guy who gossiped about Tietjens to his father. His life story is so important to the plot. UGH!<br /><br />That being said, the good parts overwhelmingly make up for the bad parts. Tietjens has a wonderful character arc - during the 3rd book I actually started to feel PROUD of him, like "You're finally figuring it all out, Chrissie. Everything is going to be okay for you." It's so great to feel that way about a character. I became VERY emotionally invested in Tietjen's and Valentine's relationship (not something I expected to happen when I picked up this book!) and the ending of the third book was one of the most satisfying endings I've ever had the pleasure of reading. It literally made me feel giddy. Which brings us to the 4th book...<br /><br />The Last Post should never have been written. Book 3 ended so perfectly, and then this half-baked 4th book comes along and leaves you scratching your head. There's good information about what happened to the characters after the war, to be sure, but it's padded with so much uninteresting filler. You spend the majority of the book reading the inner monologues of characters who were never all that important to story originally, and find yourself wondering why the hell Ford is doing this to you when all you want to know is what happens to Groby and Tietjens' little boy and Valentine. All of the plot info given to you in the 4th book could easily have been condensed into a one-chapter epilogue for book 3. The Last Post is the only reason why this is a 4-star review and not 5-star. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17190222610621832616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_793582206" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/793582206" />
    <a title="Bettie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1108123-bettie"><img alt="Bettie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602504374p2/1108123.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_2838454485" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2838454485" />
    <a title="Lady Mayfair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51675144-lady-mayfair"><img alt="Lady Mayfair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601922263p2/51675144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2838454485?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lady Mayfair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lady Mayfair" href="/user/show/51675144-lady-mayfair">Lady Mayfair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51675144-lady-mayfair?shelf=reflections">reflections</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51675144-lady-mayfair?shelf=edwardian">edwardian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51675144-lady-mayfair?shelf=longreads">longreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51675144-lady-mayfair?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51675144-lady-mayfair?shelf=twistedsisters">twistedsisters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2838454485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9475625150279519862"><i>"He had a rule: never think on a subject of a shock at the moment of a shock. The mind was then too sensitised. Subjects of shock require to be thought all year around. If your mind thinks when it is too sensitised, its then conclusions will be too strong."</i><br/><br/>This book is a gem: a war novel with little to no war, Ford dives deep into this reasoning process, of the last true Tory, using semi-neurotic streams of consciousnsess to depict the multitudinous thoughts and feelings which pass through the m</span>
  <span id="freeText9475625150279519862" style="display:none"><i>"He had a rule: never think on a subject of a shock at the moment of a shock. The mind was then too sensitised. Subjects of shock require to be thought all year around. If your mind thinks when it is too sensitised, its then conclusions will be too strong."</i><br /><br />This book is a gem: a war novel with little to no war, Ford dives deep into this reasoning process, of the last true Tory, using semi-neurotic streams of consciousnsess to depict the multitudinous thoughts and feelings which pass through the mind of all characters involved. The narrative can be a tad obtuse and confusing, but very satisfying once the reader penetrates its flow.<br /><br /><i>“There’s a picture that my mother’s got, by Burne-Jones, a cruel looking woman with a distant smile… some vampire… La Belle Dame sans Merci. That’s what you’re like.”</i><br /><br />One cannot help but love Sylvia. Ford tries terribly hard to make her detestable, but it proves impossible, despite each new cruelty. Naturally, one cannot but feel for Tietjens, but look here! To hate Sylvia is to hate Rebecca, Scarlett, Undine Spragg, Salome, Madame de Merteuil and Wanda von Dunajew. <br /><b>And I shan’t.</b></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1842051070" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1842051070" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1627591-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442492914p2/1627591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1842051070?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/1627591-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1842051070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11501645534074400562">My excuse is that the Ford Madox Ford novel on the list for my comprehensive exams as a senior in college was The Good Soldier. It was a solid WWI novel, as I recall, and that's all I recall except the distinctive name--Ford Madox Ford. So I didn't know that I didn't know about his quartet of novels, Parade's End, until I was browsing used books in Flyleaf Books in Chapel Hill and came upon all 900 pages of four novels published separately but ultimately bound together into one huge, wonderful, </span>
  <span id="freeText11501645534074400562" style="display:none">My excuse is that the Ford Madox Ford novel on the list for my comprehensive exams as a senior in college was The Good Soldier. It was a solid WWI novel, as I recall, and that's all I recall except the distinctive name--Ford Madox Ford. So I didn't know that I didn't know about his quartet of novels, Parade's End, until I was browsing used books in Flyleaf Books in Chapel Hill and came upon all 900 pages of four novels published separately but ultimately bound together into one huge, wonderful, idiosyncratic novel about Christopher Tietjens, his stiff sibling, Mark, his vicious wife, Sylvia, and his ultimate true love, Valentine...not to mention the multitude of characters that must, perforce, populate a novel that extends through pre-WWI upper-class British life, WWI in the trenches, and post-WWI British decay.<br /><br />This is a cheeky, satirical, witty, comical novel that serves as a kind of hinge between the Victorians and the Modernists. It anticipates Virginia Woolf and Henry Green. It is painfully acute in dissecting values and traditions, stuffiness and scruffiness, principle and vice. Mark is heir to Groby, a great northern estate, of no interest to him or to his younger brother. What to do? Christopher refuses to divorce his wonderful bitch wife, Silvia--because one doesn't divorce--so again, what to do? Valentine has no wherewithal to get her way no matter what she wants...what to do?<br /><br />Christopher is brilliant, idiosyncratic, and complicates everything with self-denial. He's maddening, but he's undeniably noble. The part of the novel that drags concentrates on his time in the trenches, but the way he makes things drag is also the way he preserves both his life and his virtue.<br /><br />The most surprising part of the novel is a long section wherein the stodgy, predictable Mark, having suffered a stroke, thinks through the pity of his ancestral estate and his younger brother tumbling farther and farther away from one another. Mark and Silvia, Christopher's wife, are stunningly unsentimental, which makes them stunningly shrewd and interesting. The difference between them, until the end of the novel, is that Mark is fatalistic and Silvia isn't.<br /><br />Like all great novels of a certain scope, this one has its flaws, but it also has an élan, a whispering quality, and immense insight into the rotting social structures of English life. The fun of it, if you like studies in English quirks and customs and hypocrisies, is that it goes on and on and as it does, it advises, through both good and bad examples, how to evaluate life, how to behave, how to appreciate...how to condemn... how to laugh.<br /><br />So you didn't know Parade's End by Ford Madox Ford existed? Well, now you do. It's actually as good as the high praise offered on the cover by the likes W.H. Auden and Graham Greene.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11501645534074400562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_51424804" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51424804" />
    <a title="Akemi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/297553-akemi"><img alt="Akemi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243704698p2/297553.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51424804?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Akemi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Akemi" href="/user/show/297553-akemi">Akemi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/297553-akemi?shelf=school">school</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51424804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16661365851967239752">The classic that no one has ever heard of. My professor urged us to make others read Parade's End so that it stays in print- so go read it! Technically I haven't finished it yet since we only had to read three of the four books for my class, but I will complete The Last Post soon.<br/><br/>Parade's End follows the singular Christopher Tietjens and his experiences of World War I. Tietjens is nothing if not unique, and I think it's worth reading the novel simply to meet him. Maybe I'm biased since I frequen</span>
  <span id="freeText16661365851967239752" style="display:none">The classic that no one has ever heard of. My professor urged us to make others read Parade's End so that it stays in print- so go read it! Technically I haven't finished it yet since we only had to read three of the four books for my class, but I will complete The Last Post soon.<br /><br />Parade's End follows the singular Christopher Tietjens and his experiences of World War I. Tietjens is nothing if not unique, and I think it's worth reading the novel simply to meet him. Maybe I'm biased since I frequently found myself identifying with him. Also, if you like Kant, you'd like Tietjens (as my philosopher friend who kept geeking out while reading it will attest).<br /><br />The writing style is a blend of 19th-century Victorian with 20th-century modernism, which was great for someone who really likes the 19th century and isn't that crazy about the 20th. Also, Ford is chock full of strange Britishisms, such as describing someone as "more than a little barmy on the crumpet." The British-ness of it all can make it a bit confusing at times as understanding the social mores of the time is frequently essential to getting the plot, but I guess no more so than any 19th century British novel.<br /><br />------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Finished The Last Post and was underwhelmed. Definitely not as good as the previous three books and kind of ruins the unsettled feeling you get at the end of the third book, which would be nice in another story, but unsettled works for Ford. Believe me, I am normally a big fan of the happy ending (Harry Potter Epilogue anyone?), but I found this unnecessary.<br /><br />Quotes: <br />"You seduced a young woman in order to be able to finish your talks with her. You could not do that without living with her. You could not live with her without seducing her; but that was the by-product. The point is that you can't otherwise talk. You can't finish talks at street corners; in museums; even in drawing-rooms. You mayn't be in the mood when she is in the mood - for the intimate conversation that means the final communion of your souls. You have to wait together - for a week, for a year, for a lifetime, before the final intimate conversation may be attained...and exhausted. So that...That in effect was love."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16661365851967239752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_847420346" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/847420346" />
    <a title="Joséphine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7292904-jos-phine"><img alt="Joséphine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455787382p2/7292904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/847420346?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joséphine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joséphine" href="/user/show/7292904-jos-phine">Joséphine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141392196" href="/book/show/13561867-parade-s-end">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer847420346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13544985860081177460">It took me incredibly long to get through this book and it wasn't an easy or particularly enjoying read. I totally agree with Julian Barnes who said "Ford knows more and sees deeper".<br/><br/>I found some aspects of Ford's explorations of human behaviour really interesting but it's painfully obvious to the reader that he was an incredibly detail-oriented person and to me it seems through this work, he enjoyed showing off his broad knowledge and intelligence. But honestly, do I really have to care about h</span>
  <span id="freeText13544985860081177460" style="display:none">It took me incredibly long to get through this book and it wasn't an easy or particularly enjoying read. I totally agree with Julian Barnes who said "Ford knows more and sees deeper".<br /><br />I found some aspects of Ford's explorations of human behaviour really interesting but it's painfully obvious to the reader that he was an incredibly detail-oriented person and to me it seems through this work, he enjoyed showing off his broad knowledge and intelligence. But honestly, do I really have to care about how a cidre mousseux also known as cider or apple wine is properly made? Or about the backstories of unimportant minor characters, extensively explained on countless pages, yet serving no purpose to the story whatsoever?<br /><br />Due to many time leaps within one chapter (which I thought had a very inconvenient lenght, by the way) it was easy to lose track of which character was narrating the chapter in the first place. Many pages later, when I finally got back to what was meant to be the present moment in the story, I had forgotten all about what was going on in said present. Especially Mark Tietjens' narratives were a pain to read through.<br /><br />This brings me to another point I found really unsatisfying was that due to all of these countless details, nothing really got stuck in my mind for long and I found the story to be quite forgettable in general. These are exactly the reasons why I abandoned this book several times, just to pick it up a few weeks later with a deep sigh because I rarely leave a book unfinished. Still there were certain things in the book I enjoyed, most of it being character developement or details I actually did care about because I thought they were interesting and I could relate to them. Though I'm sure this varies and depends on the reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13544985860081177460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1844462976" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1844462976" />
    <a title="Rebecca Wilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10358551-rebecca-wilson"><img alt="Rebecca Wilson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600570541p2/10358551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1844462976?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Wilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Wilson" href="/user/show/10358551-rebecca-wilson">Rebecca Wilson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10358551-rebecca-wilson?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10358551-rebecca-wilson?shelf=british">british</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1844462976" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3637998188074614747">I loved these four novels more than I ever thought possible. Well — I loved the first three; I was on the fence with the fourth. Like many, I found the fourth superfluous and a bit irritating, but ultimately worth it.<br/><br/>I was pleasantly surprised by how farcically funny and romantic these novels are. I loved the relationship between Christopher Tietjens and Valentine Wannop; though politically at odds, they are intellectually and emotionally aligned, a team of two surrounded by violence and superfi</span>
  <span id="freeText3637998188074614747" style="display:none">I loved these four novels more than I ever thought possible. Well — I loved the first three; I was on the fence with the fourth. Like many, I found the fourth superfluous and a bit irritating, but ultimately worth it.<br /><br />I was pleasantly surprised by how farcically funny and romantic these novels are. I loved the relationship between Christopher Tietjens and Valentine Wannop; though politically at odds, they are intellectually and emotionally aligned, a team of two surrounded by violence and superficiality. The humor was almost Wodehousian in its silliness — people inventing outlandish gossip for their own entertainment even in the middle of a devastating war. For example, here is a list of the things that keep Christopher and Valentine from having sex <i>for years</i> after they agree to have sex:<br />Her drunk brother<br />WWI<br />The selling of old furniture<br />Armistice Day festivities<br />A phone call from her mother<br />A band of shellshocked visitors<br />His wife pretending to have cancer<br />His wife throwing herself down the stairs<br />His brother having a stroke<br /><br />Ford doesn't shy away from depicting the violence of war, but he focuses more on Christopher's rich interior life, where he retreats to cope with the chaos around him. General Campion has to be THE most irritating person in the novel — Christopher's naive godfather who is obsessed with and deeply emotionally invested in all of the gossip surrounding Christopher, even as bombs explode around them. It's darkly humorous, but also a totally accurate depiction of human nature. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normalcy_bias" rel="nofollow noopener">We avoid facing up to terrible overwhelming disasters by fixating on bullshit.</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3637998188074614747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZUh+EU5LTkrDRa5jil+6zlirDzNuuJyHzdb1/HRorLiAFiqrRq7/eqtgQm6bx2olXxe+K0x2Gr2mVvRJ/KSIfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TeTNUD/8Gh6PJbsx0wVdnAC3SMUOr3CwIn6a1cG/n32oupnqNxmrLucAVzzCnY13Bwv53Sxh9opJ/ptgSXO7uA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

These books &quot;provide an unsurpassed picture, at once gay and melancholy, of social and artistic life in Britain between the wars&quot; (Arthur Schlesinger, Jr.). Amid the fever of the 1920s and the first chill of the 1930s, these four gain their initiations into sex, society, business, and art. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7719519577418469417">Ford Madox Ford, born Ford Hermann Hueffer, was an English novelist, poet, critic and editor whose journals, <i>The English Review</i> and <i>The Transatlantic Review</i>, were instrumental in the development of early 20th-century English literature.<br/><br/>Ford Madox Ford was the author of over 60 works: novels, poems, criticism, travel essays, and reminiscences. His work includes <i>The Good Soldier</i>, <i>Parade's End</i>, <i>The R</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7719519577418469417" style="display:none">Ford Madox Ford, born Ford Hermann Hueffer, was an English novelist, poet, critic and editor whose journals, <i>The English Review</i> and <i>The Transatlantic Review</i>, were instrumental in the development of early 20th-century English literature.<br /><br />Ford Madox Ford was the author of over 60 works: novels, poems, criticism, travel essays, and reminiscences. His work includes <i>The Good Soldier</i>, <i>Parade's End</i>, <i>The Rash Act</i>, and <i>Ladies Whose Bright Eyes</i>. He collaborated with Joseph Conrad on <i>The Inheritors</i>, <i>Romance</i>, and other works. Ford lived in both France and the United States and died in 1939.</span>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Higher than the beasts, lower than the angels, stuck in our idiot Eden.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/617744-higher-than-the-beasts-lower-than-the-angels-stuck-in">104 likes</a>
            </nobr>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You seduced a young woman in order to be able to finish your talks with her. You could not do that without living with her. You could not live with her without seducing her; but that was the by-product. The point is that you can't otherwise talk. You can't finish talks at street corners; in museums; even in drawing-rooms. You mayn't be in the mood when she is in the mood – for the intimate conversation that means the final communion of your souls. You have to wait together – for a week, for a year, for a lifetime, before the final intimate conversation may be attained...and exhausted. So that...<br /><br />That in effect was love.&rdquo;</span>
